<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg/220px-Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg/330px-Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg/440px-Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg 2x" data-file-width="672" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hereford_Mappa_Mundi_1300.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hereford Mappa Mundi with the isles in the lower left hand corner.</div></div></div>
<p>While Latin remained the language of learning, from the early <a href="/wiki/Mediaeval" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediaeval">mediaeval</a> period records begin to appear in native languages. The earliest <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> source to use a collective term for the archipelago is the <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_of_Saint_Columba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Life of Saint Columba (page does not exist)">Life of Saint Columba</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a> recording the missionary activities of the sixth century Irish monk <a href="/wiki/Saint_Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Columba">Saint Columba</a> among the peoples of what is now Scotland. It was written in the late seventh century by <a href="/wiki/Adomn%C3%A1n_of_Iona" class="mw-redirect" title="Adomnán of Iona">Adomnán</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a>, an Irish monk living on the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebridean" class="mw-redirect" title="Inner Hebridean">Inner Hebridean</a> island. The collective term for the archipelago used within this work is <b>Oceani Insulae</b> meaning "Islands of the Ocean" (Book 2, 46 in the Sharpe edition = Book 2, 47 in Reeves edition), it is used sparingly and no <i>Priteni</i>-derived collective reference is made.
</p><p>Another early native source to use a collective term is the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> written in the early eighth century. The collective term for the archipelago used within this work is <i>insularum</i> meaning "islands" (Book 1, 8) and it too is used sparingly. He stated that Britain "studies and confesses one and the same knowledge of the highest truth in the tongues of five nations, namely the Angles, the Britons, the Scots, the Picts, and the Latins", distinguishing between the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> of the "ancient Britons" or <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a> speakers and other language groups.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Brythonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Saxon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_York" title="Scandinavian York">Jórvík</a> amalgamated, leading to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> was sometimes united under princes or kings such as <a href="/wiki/Gruffydd_ap_Llywelyn" title="Gruffydd ap Llywelyn">Gruffydd ap Llywelyn</a>. Between 854 and 1171, a kingship of Ireland was established by kings of the regional kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1el_Sechnaill_mac_M%C3%A1ele_Ruanaid" title="Máel Sechnaill mac Máele Ruanaid">Máel Sechnaill mac Máele Ruanaid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toirdelbach_Ua_Briain" title="Toirdelbach Ua Briain">Toirdelbach Ua Briain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muirchertach_Mac_Lochlainn" title="Muirchertach Mac Lochlainn">Muirchertach Mac Lochlainn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ruaidr%C3%AD_Ua_Conchobair" title="Ruaidrí Ua Conchobair">Ruaidrí Ua Conchobair</a>, something not achieved in Britain until 1707. In subsequent <a href="/wiki/Norman_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Ireland">Norman Ireland</a>, local lords gained considerable autonomy from the <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a> until it became the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> under direct English rule.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_mapmakers">Renaissance mapmakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Renaissance mapmakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg/220px-Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg/330px-Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg/440px-Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6083" data-file-height="5134" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blaeu_-_Atlas_of_Scotland_1654_-_INSULAE_ALBION_ET_HIBERNIA_-_Old_Great_Britain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1654 Blaeu Atlas of Scotland, <i>Insulae Albion Et Hibernia</i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ortelius" title="Abraham Ortelius">Abraham Ortelius</a> makes clear his understanding that England, Scotland and Ireland were politically separate in 1570  by the full title of his map: <i>Angliae, Scotiae et Hiberniae, sive Britannicar. insularum descriptio</i> ('A representation of England, Scotland and Ireland, or the Britannic islands'). <a href="/wiki/George_Lily" title="George Lily">George Lily</a>'s 1546 map divides Britain into the two kingdoms of England and Scotland, with Ireland alongside. Some maps from this period also appear to mark Wales, and sometimes Cornwall, as separate areas within Britain, while the history of England created by <a href="/wiki/Polydore_Vergil" title="Polydore Vergil">Polydore Vergil</a><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> states, "The whole country of Britain is divided into four parts, whereof the one is inhabited by Englishmen, the other of Scots, the third Welshmen and the fourth of Cornish people."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Maps of the Mediaeval, Renaissance and later periods often referred to <i><a href="/wiki/Albion" title="Albion">Albion</a></i>. This archaic term was originally used by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> to mean the island of Great Britain. In later centuries its meaning changed to refer only to the area we now call Scotland (<i>Albany</i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Alba" title="Alba">Alba</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Gaelic language">Gaelic</a>). <i>Albion</i> has survived as a poetic name for Britain but it is not in everyday use.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_and_19th_centuries">18th and 19th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 18th and 19th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png/220px-Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png/330px-Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png/440px-Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png 2x" data-file-width="2144" data-file-height="1224" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Moll_-_The_north_part_of_Great_Britain_called_Scotland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1726 map showing "The north part of Great Britain called Scotland"</div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a>, a fashion arose, particularly in Scotland, for referring to Scotland and England as <i><a href="/wiki/North_Britain" title="North Britain">North Britain</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/South_Britain" title="South Britain">South Britain</a></i> respectively. These terms gained in popularity during the 19th century. The most lasting example of this usage was in the name of the <a href="/wiki/North_British_Railway" title="North British Railway">North British Railway</a>, which became part of the <a href="/wiki/London_and_North_Eastern_Railway" title="London and North Eastern Railway">London and North Eastern Railway</a> in 1923, and in the name of the North British Hotel, built by the railway in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> in 1902, which retained the name until it reopened in 1991 as the Balmoral Hotel.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_kingdoms_and_states">Evolution of kingdoms and states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Evolution of kingdoms and states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nations_of_the_UK.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Nations_of_the_UK.png/400px-Nations_of_the_UK.png" width="400" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Nations_of_the_UK.png/600px-Nations_of_the_UK.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Nations_of_the_UK.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nations_of_the_UK.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A timeline of <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">states</a> in the British Isles. (<i>Ireland</i> continues to exist, but the description "Republic of Ireland" is used as a disambiguator in this case).</div></div></div>
<p>The diagram on the right gives an indication of the further evolution of kingdoms and states. In 1603 the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_King" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish King">Scottish King</a> <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James VI</a> inherited the <a href="/wiki/English_throne" class="mw-redirect" title="English throne">English throne</a> as "James I of England". He styled himself as <i>James I of Great Britain</i>, although both states retained their sovereignty and independent parliaments, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>. (The term "Great Britain" itself reportedly dates from as early as 1474, and was in common usage from the mid-16th century onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>)
</p><p>The 1707 <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1707">Act of Union</a> united England and Scotland in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a>, then in 1800 Ireland was brought under British government control by the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union</a> creating the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>. Irish unrest culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish War">Irish War of Independence</a> and the 1922 separation of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, which later became a republic with the name Ireland. The majority Protestant <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">northeast</a> continued to be part of what became the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/British_overseas_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="British overseas territories">British overseas territories</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> have various relationships with the UK. The <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, initially formalised in 1931 (the British Commonwealth until 1949), is an association of independent states roughly corresponding to the former <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. (This has no connection with the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a>, a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> replacing the previous kingdoms during the <a href="/wiki/English_Interregnum" class="mw-redirect" title="English Interregnum">English Interregnum</a> (1649–1660).)
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">Adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Adjectives" class="mw-redirect" title="Adjectives">adjectives</a> used to describe the contents and attributes of the various constituent parts of the British Isles also cause confusion.
</p><p>In the absence of a single adjective to refer to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <i>British</i> is generally used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> as a whole. However, in a specifically physical geographical sense, <i>British</i> is used to refer to the island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>  The adjectival phrase <i>Great British</i> is very rarely used to refer to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, other than to contrive a <a href="/wiki/Pun" title="Pun">pun</a> on the word <i>great</i>, as in "Great British Food".
</p><p><i>Irish</i>, refers to people or a characteristic "of Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  As such, its meaning is contextual on the meaning of "Ireland" being used: it can relate both to the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Irish state</a>, and to the <a href="/wiki/Ireland_(island)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland (island)">island of Ireland</a>. <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, as a constituent part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, can thus be both <i>British</i> or <i>Irish</i>, reflected in the ability  for residents of Northern Ireland to take either British or Irish citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In order to be more specific, <i>Northern Irish</i> is therefore in common usage. Members of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Nationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Nationalism">Nationalist</a> communities would not describe themselves as <i>British</i> and would only use the terms <i>Irish</i>, or specifically <i>Northern Irish</i> where needed.
</p><p>The term <i>Ulster</i> can also be used as an adjective (e.g. "<a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a>"), but this is more likely to be used by <a href="/wiki/Unionists_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionists (Ireland)">Unionists</a> and has political connotations in the same fashion as its use as a proper noun (because only six of the traditional nine counties of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">Antrim</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Armagh" title="County Armagh">Armagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">Down</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Fermanagh" title="County Fermanagh">Fermanagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Londonderry" title="County Londonderry">Londonderry</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Tyrone" title="County Tyrone">Tyrone</a>, are included in Northern Ireland with the remaining three counties <a href="/wiki/County_Cavan" title="County Cavan">Cavan</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">Donegal</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Monaghan" title="County Monaghan">Monaghan</a> forming part of the Republic).  The term <i>Ulsterman</i> (or <i>Ulsterwoman</i>) is common and holds no such political connotation. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Nationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Nationalism">Nationalists</a> might describe, say, a lake in Northern Ireland as <i>Irish</i>.
</p><p>Note that the geographical term <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> thus far appears to have escaped political connotations, even though territorial control of the waters of the Irish Sea is divided between both the Republic of Ireland and the UK, and also includes a British <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependency" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown dependency">Crown dependency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>—as yet there appears to be no controversy with the term’s usage to mirror that of "British Isles". The "Northern" in "Northern Ireland" is not completely accurate.  The most northerly point on the island, <a href="/wiki/Malin_Head" title="Malin Head">Malin Head</a>, is in the Republic of Ireland—in <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">County Donegal</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Inishowen" title="Inishowen">Inishowen</a> Peninsula.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_with_use_of_terms">Problems with use of terms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Problems with use of terms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Isles">British Isles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: British Isles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Isles_naming_dispute" title="British Isles naming dispute">British Isles naming dispute</a></div>
<p>The dictionary definition of <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> is that it is a geographical term that refers to the whole of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> as well as the surrounding islands. It is sometimes incorrectly used as if identical to the UK;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or to refer to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and the surrounding islands, excluding the island of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> have style guides that mandate the dictionary definition but occasional misuse can be found on their web sites.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a></i> can also be considered irritating or offensive by some<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> on the grounds that the modern association of the term <i>British</i> with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> makes its application to Ireland inappropriate.
</p><p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a></i> can also be considered to imply a proprietary title on the entire archipelago.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The policy of the government of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> is that no branch of government should use the term,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and although it is on occasion used in a geographical sense in Irish parliamentary debates, this is often done in a way that excludes the Republic of Ireland. In October 2006, <i>The Times</i> quoted a spokesman for the Irish Embassy in London as saying that they would discourage its use.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During a stop-over visit to the Republic of Ireland in 1989, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, indicated that he assumed Ireland's <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> was <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>, given that she was the British Queen and his officials said that Ireland was a part of the British Isles.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">nationalists</a> reject the term and instead use <i>these islands</i>, <i>these isles</i> or "Britain and Ireland" as an alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been several suggestions for replacements for the term <i>British Isles</i>. Although there is no single accepted replacement, the terms <i>Great Britain and Ireland</i>, <i>The British Isles and Ireland</i> and <i>Britain and Ireland</i> are all used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg/240px-WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg" width="240" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg/360px-WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg/480px-WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WWII_Divide_and_Conquer_12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A still from the 1943 US propaganda film series <i><a href="/wiki/Why_We_Fight" title="Why We Fight">Why We Fight</a></i>, which suggests that the name "England" applies to the whole of Great Britain</div></div></div>
<p>The word "England" is often used synecdochically to refer to Great Britain—or the United Kingdom as a whole<sup id="cite_ref-gmikes_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmikes-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jrose_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jrose-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>—which often causes offence, particularly to those from the non-English parts of Britain. In a similar way, references to England as an island,<sup id="cite_ref-islandofEngland_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-islandofEngland-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> to an "English passport",<sup id="cite_ref-englandpassport_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-englandpassport-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> or to Scottish or Welsh places as being in England<sup id="cite_ref-englandpassport_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-englandpassport-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scotlandengland_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotlandengland-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> are examples of this usage of the term "England".
</p><p>Because of the offence likely to be taken by Scots, Welsh and Irish at this usage, most politicians and official figures have avoided this usage since the early 20th century. However, there are frequent examples of this usage from earlier times.<sup id="cite_ref-nelson_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nelson-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-disraeli_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disraeli-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mlincoln_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlincoln-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> For a long time it was common for fans of the <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England football team</a> to wave the British <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a>—with the use of the specifically English <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="St George&#39;s Cross">St George's Cross</a> flag only gaining popularity at the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_96" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA Euro 96">Euro 96</a> tournament.<sup id="cite_ref-englandpassport_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-englandpassport-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The colloquial usage of "England" as a synonym for "Britain" is still widespread outside the UK. In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the term "England" is often used to mean Great Britain or even the entire United Kingdom. In many other languages, such as Chinese, Japanese or Korean, the word for "English" is synonymous with "British"—see the article on <a href="/wiki/Alternative_words_for_British#Other_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Alternative words for British">Alternative words for British</a> for more detail.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>" may be used in one of several different contexts by British and Irish people: either to refer to the whole of the European continent, to refer to only to <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Mainland Europe">Mainland Europe</a>, sometimes called "continental Europe" or simply "the Continent" by some people in the archipelago. <i>Europe</i> may also be used in reference to the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (or, historically, to the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>). A comedic treatment of the different uses of this word appears in an episode of the BBC sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/To_the_Manor_Born" title="To the Manor Born">To the Manor Born</a></i>. When tradesmen are taking measurements in metric, and Audrey fforbes-Hamilton objects on the grounds that the house was built "in feet and inches", a tradesman says "We're in Europe now", referring to the European Economic Community. Audrey fforbes-Hamilton retorts "Well you may be, but I'm staying here!" - implying that to her, the word "Europe" referred only to mainland Europe, excluding Britain and Ireland.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Britain_2">Great Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Great Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word "Great" means "larger", in comparison with <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.  One historical term for the <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a> in France that largely corresponds to the modern French province is <i>Lesser</i> or <i>Little Britain</i>.  That region was settled by many British immigrants during the period of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> migration into Britain, and named "Little Britain" by them. The <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> term "Bretagne" now refers to the French "Little Britain", not to the British "Great Britain", which in French is called <i>Grande-Bretagne</i>. In classical times, the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Graeco-Roman</a> geographer <a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Ptolemy</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i> also called the larger island <i>megale Brettania</i> (great Britain). At that time, it was in contrast to the smaller island of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, which he called <i>mikra Brettania</i> (little Britain).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In his later work <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i>, Ptolemy refers to Great Britain as <i>Albion</i> and to Ireland as <i><a href="/wiki/Hibernia" title="Hibernia">Iwernia</a></i>. These "new" names were likely to have been the native names for the islands at the time. The earlier names, <a href="/wiki/Exonym_and_endonym" title="Exonym and endonym">in contrast</a>, were likely to have been coined before direct contact with local peoples was made.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>Britain</i> is ambiguous, being used variously to mean Great Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-Britain-CED_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britain-CED-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britain-OLD_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britain-OLD-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britain-Col_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britain-Col-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-Britain-MW_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britain-MW-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britain-Col_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britain-Col-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and for some, England.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Use of <i>Britain</i> can be contentious, with many people in Northern Ireland objecting to its application to their province.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> While some organisations including the BBC and British Government prefer to use <i>Britain</i> as shorthand for Great Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-checklist-bbc_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-checklist-bbc-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-style-guide-govuk_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-style-guide-govuk-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> others prefer, where precision is not required, to use <i>Britain</i> to mean the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland_3">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>Ireland</i> has two meanings.
</p>
<ol><li>It is the official name of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">the state</a> that occupies five sixths of the island, formally named the Republic of Ireland.</li>
<li>It is a geographical term for <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">the whole island</a>, which may be referred to as "the island of Ireland" to avoid ambiguity.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ulster">Ulster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ulster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IrelandUlster.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/IrelandUlster.svg/220px-IrelandUlster.svg.png" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/IrelandUlster.svg/330px-IrelandUlster.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/IrelandUlster.svg/440px-IrelandUlster.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IrelandUlster.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The traditional province of Ulster on the island of Ireland, showing the modern-day border between the Republic of Ireland and Northern Ireland</div></div></div>
<p>The terminology and usage of the name <i>Ulster</i> in Irish and British culture varies. Many within the <a href="/wiki/Unionist_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionist (Ireland)">unionist</a> community<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and much of the UK press refer to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> as <i>Ulster</i>&#160;&#8211;&#32;whereas the <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">nationalist</a> community refer to the traditional <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Ireland" title="Provinces of Ireland">Irish province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, which is a nine-county entity that incorporates the three counties of <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">Donegal</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Cavan" title="County Cavan">Cavan</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Monaghan" title="County Monaghan">Monaghan</a>  (which are in the Republic) along with the counties of <a href="/wiki/County_Armagh" title="County Armagh">Armagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">Antrim</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">Down</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Fermanagh" title="County Fermanagh">Fermanagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Londonderry" title="County Londonderry">Londonderry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyrone" title="Tyrone">Tyrone</a> in Northern Ireland.
</p><p>Thus, the word <i>Ulster</i> has two usages:
</p>
<ol><li>It is the name of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">one</a> of the four <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Ireland" title="Provinces of Ireland">Provinces of Ireland</a>, consisting of the nine northern counties of the island, that was partitioned between the United Kingdom (six counties) and the Republic of Ireland (three counties).</li>
<li>It is an alternative name for <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, used by many in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Unionism">Unionist</a> community.  It consists of the six north-eastern counties of the island that remain part of the United Kingdom.</li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_information">Further information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Further information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isle_of_Man_and_Channel_Islands">Isle of Man and Channel Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Isle of Man and Channel Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Isle of Man and the two <a href="/wiki/Bailiwick" title="Bailiwick">bailiwicks</a> of the Channel Islands are <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a>; that is, non-sovereign <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a>, self-governing but whose sovereignty is held by the British <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">Crown</a>. They control their own internal affairs, but not their defence or foreign relations. They are not part of the United Kingdom or part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> is part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, situated in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> between Great Britain and Ireland.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a> consist politically of two self-governing <a href="/wiki/Bailiwick" title="Bailiwick">bailiwicks</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Bailiwick_of_Guernsey" title="Bailiwick of Guernsey">Bailiwick of Guernsey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Bailiwick of Jersey</a>. They are the remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Duchy of Normandy</a>, which was once in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> with the Kingdom of England. They are sometimes, despite their location next to mainland <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, considered part of the British Isles.  This usage is political rather than geographic.</li>
<li>The Isle of Man and the Channel Islands are <a href="/wiki/British_Islands" title="British Islands">British Islands</a> in United Kingdom law.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celtic_names">Celtic names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Celtic names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are five living <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a> in the region. Each has names for the islands and countries of the British Isles. They are divided into two branches:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic</a> – which includes <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic</a> – which includes <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Gaelic language">Scottish Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a></li></ul>
<p>Some of the above are:
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable">

<tbody><tr>
<th><b>English</b>
</th>
<th>Cornwall
</th>
<th>Wales
</th>
<th>Ireland
</th>
<th>Northern<br />Ireland
</th>

<p>The English word <i>Welsh</i> is from a common <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> root meaning "Romanised foreigner" (<a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">Wallonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a>, and also cognate with the word used in Mediaeval German to refer to the French and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italians</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Wales_Hist_69_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wales_Hist_69-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The English names <i><a href="/wiki/Albion" title="Albion">Albion</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Albany</a></i> are related to <i>Alba</i> and used poetically for either England or Scotland, or the whole island of Great Britain.
</p><p>English <i><a href="/wiki/Erin" title="Erin">Erin</a></i> is a poetic name for Ireland derived from <i>Éire</i> (or rather, from its dative form <i>Éirinn</i>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terms_for_the_British_Isles_in_the_Irish_language">Terms for the British Isles in the Irish language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Terms for the British Isles in the Irish language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Irish, the term <i>Oileáin Bhriotanacha</i> is a translation of the English term <i>British Isles</i>. Another translation is <i>Oileáin Bhreataineacha</i>, which was used in the 1937 translation from English to Irish of a 1931 geography book.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Earlier dictionaries<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> give <i>Oileáin Iarthair Eorpa</i> as the translation, literally meaning <i>West European Isles</i>. Today the most common term <i>Éire agus an Bhreatain Mhór</i> is used, meaning literally as <i>Ireland and Great Britain</i>, as provided by terminological dictionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-tearma_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tearma-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Slang">Slang</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Terminology_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Slang">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Blighty" title="Blighty">Blighty</a></i> is a slang word for Britain derived from the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a> word <i>bilāyatī</i> ("foreign"). Depending on the user, it is meant either affectionately or archly. It was often used by British soldiers abroad in the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> to refer to home.
</p>

It is without any official status. The Government, including the Department of Foreign Affairs, does not use this term. Our officials in the Embassy of Ireland, London, continue to monitor the media in Britain for any abuse of the official terms as set out in the Constitution of Ireland and in legislation. These include the name of the State, the President, <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a> and others."</span>

<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Tír-Eóluíocht na h-Éireann</i> (translation by Toirdhealbhach Ó Raithbheartaigh of <i>Macmillan's General and Regional Geography of Ireland</i> by T. J. Dunne), Government Publications Office, Dublin<blockquote><p><i>Tá Éire ar cheann de na h-oileáin a dtugar na <u>h-Oileáin Bhreataineacha</u> ortha agus atá ar an taobh Thiar-Thuaidh de'n Eóraip. Tá siad tuairim a's ar chúig mhíle oileán ar fad ann. (Oileánradh an t-ainm a bheirtear ar áit ar bith i n-a bhfuil a lán oileán agus iad i n-aice a chéile mar seo.) Éire agus an Bhreatain Mhór (Sasain, an Bhreatain Bheag, agus Alba) an dá oileán is mó de na h-Oileáin Bhreataineacha.</i></p></blockquote><blockquote><p>Ireland is one of the islands which are called the British Isles and which are on the North-Western side of Europe. It is thought that there are five thousand islands in total there. (Archipelago is the name which is borne by a place in which there are many islands next to each other like these.) Ireland and Great Britain (England, Wales, and Scotland) are the two largest islands of the British Isles.</p></blockquote></span>
